<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Horses in warfare</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"War horse" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/War_Horse_(disambiguation)" title="War Horse (disambiguation)">War Horse (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="572" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg/220px-Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg/330px-Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg/440px-Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scotland_Forever_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Scotland_Forever!" title="Scotland Forever!">Scotland Forever!</a></i> depicting the charge of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots_Greys" title="Royal Scots Greys">Royal Scots Greys</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first use of <b>horses in warfare</b> occurred over 5,000 years ago. The earliest evidence of horses <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">ridden</a> in warfare dates from <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> between 4000 and 3000 BC. A <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> illustration of warfare from 2500 BC depicts some type of <a href="/wiki/Equidae" title="Equidae">equine</a> pulling wagons. By 1600 BC, improved <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">harness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> designs made chariot warfare common throughout the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>, and the earliest written training manual for war horses was a guide for training chariot horses written about 1350 BC. As formal <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> tactics replaced the chariot, so did new training methods, and by 360 BC, the Greek cavalry officer <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> had written an extensive treatise on horsemanship. The effectiveness of horses in battle was also revolutionized by improvements in <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>, including the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">saddle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">stirrup</a>, and later, the <a href="/wiki/Horse_collar" title="Horse collar">horse collar</a>.</p>
<p>Many different types and sizes of horse were used in war, depending on the form of warfare. The type used varied with whether the horse was being ridden or driven, and whether they were being used for <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>, cavalry charges, <a href="/wiki/Raid_(military)" title="Raid (military)">raiding</a>, communication, or supply. Throughout history, <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">mules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a> as well as horses played a crucial role in providing support to armies in the field.</p>
<p>Horses were well suited to the warfare tactics of the <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> cultures from the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. Several <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> cultures made extensive use of cavalry and chariots. <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> warriors relied upon <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a> in their campaigns throughout <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, and Europe beginning in the 7th and 8th centuries AD. Europeans used several types of war horses in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, and the best-known <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a> warrior of the period was the armoured <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knight</a>. With the decline of the knight and rise of gunpowder in warfare, light cavalry again rose to prominence, used in both European warfare and in the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. Battle cavalry developed to take on a multitude of roles in the late 18th century and early 19th century and was often crucial for victory in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a>. In the Americas, the use of horses and development of mounted warfare tactics were learned by several tribes of <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous people</a> and in turn, highly mobile horse regiments were critical in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.</p>
<p>Horse cavalry began to be phased out after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> in favour of <a href="/wiki/Armoured_warfare" title="Armoured warfare">tank warfare</a>, though a few horse cavalry units were still used into <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, especially as scouts. By the end of World War II, horses were seldom seen in battle, but were still used extensively for the transport of troops and supplies. Today, formal battle ready horse cavalry units have almost disappeared, though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Special_Forces" title="United States Army Special Forces">United States Army Special Forces</a> used horses in battle during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2001_invasion_of_Afghanistan" title="2001 invasion of Afghanistan">2001 invasion of Afghanistan</a>. Horses are still seen in use by organized armed fighters in <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> countries. Many nations still maintain small units of mounted riders for patrol and <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>, and military horse units are also used for ceremonial and educational purposes. Horses are also used for <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical reenactment</a> of battles, <a href="/wiki/Mounted_police" title="Mounted police">law enforcement</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrian</a> competitions derived from the riding and training skills once used by the military.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_horse_used_in_warfare">Types of horse used in warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Types of horse used in warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A fundamental principle of <a href="/wiki/Equine_conformation" title="Equine conformation">equine conformation</a> is "form to function". Therefore, the type of horse used for various forms of warfare depended on the work performed, the weight a horse needed to carry or pull, and distance travelled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Weight affects speed and endurance, creating a trade-off: armour added protection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> but added weight reduces maximum speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Therefore, various cultures had different military needs. In some situations, one primary type of horse was favoured over all others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In other places, multiple types were needed; warriors would travel to battle riding a lighter horse of greater speed and endurance, and then switch to a heavier horse, with greater weight-carrying capacity, when wearing heavy armour in actual combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NicolleCK14_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-NicolleCK14-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The average horse can carry up to approximately 30% of its body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> While all horses can pull more than they can carry, the weight horses can pull varies widely, depending on the build of the horse, the type of vehicle, road conditions, and other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotner1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cotner1-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Horses <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">harnessed</a> to a wheeled vehicle on a paved road can pull as much as eight times their weight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dynamometer_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-dynamometer-10">[10]</a></sup> but far less if pulling wheelless loads over unpaved terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Thus, horses that were <a href="/wiki/Driving_(horse)" title="Driving (horse)">driven</a> varied in size and had to make a trade-off between speed and weight, just as did riding animals. Light horses could pull a small war chariot at speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Heavy supply wagons, <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a>, and support vehicles were pulled by heavier horses or a larger number of horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin146_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin146-14">[14]</a></sup> The method by which a horse was hitched to a vehicle also mattered: horses could pull greater weight with a <a href="/wiki/Horse_collar" title="Horse collar">horse collar</a> than they could with a <a href="/wiki/Breastplate_(tack)" title="Breastplate (tack)">breast collar</a>, and even less with an ox <a href="/wiki/Yoke" title="Yoke">yoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin106_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin106-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light-weight">Light-weight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Light-weight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4241" data-file-width="4966" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edouard_Detaille_-_Vive_L%27Empereur_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hussar" title="Hussar">Hussars</a> were a <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light type of cavalry</a> between the 15th and 19th century. Here, <a href="/wiki/4th_Hussar_Regiment_(France)" title="4th Hussar Regiment (France)">French hussars</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Light, <a href="/wiki/Oriental_horse" title="Oriental horse">oriental horses</a> such as the ancestors of the modern <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barb_(horse)" title="Barb (horse)">Barb</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Akhal-Teke" title="Akhal-Teke">Akhal-Teke</a> were used for warfare that required speed, endurance and agility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Such horses ranged from about 12 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (48 inches, 122 cm) to just under 15 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (60 inches, 152 cm), weighing approximately 800 to 1,000 pounds (360 to 450 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> To move quickly, riders had to use lightweight <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">tack</a> and carry relatively light weapons such as bows, light spears, javelins, or, later, rifles. This was the original horse used for early chariot warfare, raiding, and light <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Relatively light horses were used by many cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arabs</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a>. Throughout the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>, small, light animals were used to pull chariots designed to carry no more than two passengers, a driver and a warrior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett29_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett29-22">[22]</a></sup> In the European <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, a lightweight war horse became known as the <a href="/wiki/Rouncey" title="Rouncey">rouncey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medium-weight">Medium-weight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medium-weight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mooko-HakataWall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="234" data-file-width="585" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mooko-HakataWall.jpg/220px-Mooko-HakataWall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mooko-HakataWall.jpg/330px-Mooko-HakataWall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Mooko-HakataWall.jpg/440px-Mooko-HakataWall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mooko-HakataWall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arriving Japanese <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> prepares to man the fortification against invaders of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a>, painted c. 1293 AD. By this time, a medium-weight horse was used.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Medium-weight horses developed as early as the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> with the needs of various civilizations to pull heavier loads, such as chariots capable of holding more than two people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett29_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett29-22">[22]</a></sup> and, as <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a> evolved into <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a>, to carry heavily armoured riders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards11ff_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards11ff-24">[24]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> were among the earliest cultures to produce taller, heavier horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Larger horses were also needed to pull supply wagons and, later on, artillery pieces. In Europe, horses were also used to a limited extent to maneuver <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannons" title="Cannons">cannons</a> on the battlefield as part of dedicated <a href="/wiki/Horse_artillery" title="Horse artillery">horse artillery</a> units. Medium-weight horses had the greatest range in size, from about 14.2 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (58 inches, 147 cm) but stocky,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards11ff_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards11ff-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland85_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland85-26">[26]</a></sup> to as much as 16 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (64 inches, 163 cm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> weighing approximately 1,000 to 1,200 pounds (450 to 540 kg). They generally were quite agile in combat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> though they did not have the raw speed or endurance of a lighter horse. By the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, larger horses in this class were sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Destrier" title="Destrier">destriers</a>. They may have resembled modern <a href="/wiki/Baroque_horse" title="Baroque horse">Baroque</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heavy_warmblood" title="Heavy warmblood">heavy warmblood</a> breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[note 1]</a></sup> Later, horses similar to the modern <a href="/wiki/Warmblood" title="Warmblood">warmblood</a> often carried European cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDF_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDF-31">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heavy-weight">Heavy-weight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Heavy-weight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large, heavy horses, weighing from 1,500 to 2,000 pounds (680 to 910 kg), the ancestors of today's <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draught horses</a>, were used, particularly in Europe, from the Middle Ages onward. They pulled heavy loads like supply wagons and were disposed to remain calm in battle. Some historians believe they may have carried the heaviest-armoured <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a> of the European Late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, though others dispute this claim, indicating that the destrier, or knight's battle horse, was a medium-weight animal. It is also disputed whether the destrier class included draught animals or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Breeds at the smaller end of the heavyweight category may have included the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Percheron" title="Percheron">Percheron</a>, agile for their size and physically able to manoeuvre in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ponies">Ponies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ponies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The British Army's 2nd Dragoons in 1813 had 340 ponies of 14.2 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (58 inches, 147 cm) and 55 ponies of 14 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (56 inches, 142 cm);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> the Lovat Scouts, formed in 1899, were mounted on Highland ponies;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> the British Army recruited 200 Dales ponies in World War II for use as pack and artillery animals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> and the British Territorial Army experimented with the use of Dartmoor ponies as <a href="/wiki/Pack_animal" title="Pack animal">pack animals</a> in 1935, finding them to be better than mules for the job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_equids">Other equids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other equids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Horses were not the only <a href="/wiki/Equidae" title="Equidae">equids</a> used to support human warfare. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donkeys" title="Donkeys">Donkeys</a> have been used as pack animals from antiquity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> to the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">Mules</a> were also commonly used, especially as pack animals and to pull wagons, but also occasionally for riding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbell_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbell-40">[39]</a></sup> Because mules are often both calmer and hardier than horses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genetics190_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genetics190-41">[40]</a></sup> they were particularly useful for strenuous support tasks, such as hauling supplies over difficult terrain. However, under gunfire, they were less cooperative than horses, so were generally not used to haul <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> on battlefields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotner1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cotner1-8">[8]</a></sup> The size of a mule and work to which it was put depended largely on the breeding of the <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mare</a> that produced the mule. Mules could be lightweight, medium weight, or even, when produced from draught horse <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mares</a>, of moderate heavy weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ensminger85_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ensminger85-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Training_and_deployment">Training and deployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Training and deployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Horse_training" title="Horse training">Horse training</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2966" data-file-width="2225" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg/220px-Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg/330px-Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg/440px-Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kastor_Niobid_krater_Louvre_G341.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Castor_and_Pollux" title="Castor and Pollux">Castor</a> wearing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilos" title="Pilos">pilos</a>-like helmet, detail from a scene representing the gathering of the <a href="/wiki/Argonauts" title="Argonauts">Argonauts</a>, from an Attic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-figure" title="Red-figure">red-figure</a> calyx-<a href="/wiki/Krater" title="Krater">krater</a>, ca. 460–450 BC, <a href="/wiki/Orvieto" title="Orvieto">Orvieto</a> (<a href="/wiki/Volsinii" title="Volsinii">Volsinii</a>), Italy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest known manual on training horses for chariot warfare was written c. 1350 BC by the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> horsemaster, <a href="/wiki/Kikkuli" title="Kikkuli">Kikkuli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin48_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin48-43">[42]</a></sup> An ancient manual on the subject of training riding horses, particularly for the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> is <i>Hippike</i> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_Horsemanship_(Xenophon)" title="On Horsemanship (Xenophon)">On Horsemanship</a></i>) written about 360 BC by the Greek cavalry officer <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup> One of the earliest texts from Asia was that of <a href="/wiki/Chanakya" title="Chanakya">Kautilya</a>, written about 323 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin48_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin48-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whether horses were trained to pull chariots, to be ridden as light or heavy cavalry, or to carry the armoured knight, much training was required to overcome the horse's natural instinct to flee from noise, the smell of blood, and the confusion of combat. They also learned to accept any sudden or unusual movements of humans while using a weapon or avoiding one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland115_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland115-45">[44]</a></sup> Horses used in close combat may have been taught, or at least permitted, to kick, strike, and even bite, thus becoming weapons themselves for the warriors they carried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gravett29_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gravett29-46">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>In most cultures, a war horse used as a riding animal was trained to be controlled with limited use of <a href="/wiki/Rein" title="Rein">reins</a>, responding primarily to the rider's <a href="/wiki/Riding_aids" title="Riding aids">legs and weight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Au_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Au-47">[46]</a></sup> The horse became accustomed to any necessary <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">tack</a> and protective armour placed upon it, and learned to balance under a rider who would also be laden with weapons and armour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland115_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland115-45">[44]</a></sup> Developing the balance and agility of the horse was crucial. The origins of the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a> came from the need to train horses to be both obedient and manoeuvrable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDF_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-USDF-31">[30]</a></sup> The <i>Haute ecole</i> or "High School" movements of <a href="/wiki/Classical_dressage" title="Classical dressage">classical dressage</a> taught today at the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Riding_School" title="Spanish Riding School">Spanish Riding School</a> have their roots in manoeuvres designed for the battlefield. However, the <i><a href="/wiki/Airs_above_the_ground" title="Airs above the ground">airs above the ground</a></i> were unlikely to have been used in actual combat, as most would have exposed the unprotected underbelly of the horse to the weapons of foot soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin197_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin197-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Horses used for <a href="/wiki/Chariot_tactics" title="Chariot tactics">chariot warfare</a> were not only trained for combat conditions, but because many chariots were pulled by a team of two to four horses, they also had to learn to work together with other animals in close quarters under chaotic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roman214_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roman214-49">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technological_innovations">Technological innovations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Technological innovations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Horses were probably ridden in prehistory before they were driven. However, evidence is scant, mostly simple images of human figures on horse-like animals drawn on rock or clay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trench_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trench-51">[50]</a></sup> The earliest tools used to control horses were <a href="/wiki/Bridle" title="Bridle">bridles</a> of various sorts, which were invented nearly as soon as the horse was <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domesticated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Bit_(horse)" title="Bit (horse)">bit</a> wear appears on the teeth of horses excavated at the archaeology sites of the <a href="/wiki/Botai_culture" title="Botai culture">Botai culture</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, dated 3500–3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartwick_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartwick-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harness_and_vehicles">Harness and vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Harness and vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1432" data-file-width="2048" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg/220px-%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg/330px-%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg/440px-%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptischer_Maler_um_1355_v._Chr._001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">Chariots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archers</a> were weapons of war in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The invention of the wheel was a major technological innovation that gave rise to <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> warfare. At first, equines, both horses and <a href="/wiki/Asinus" title="Asinus">onagers</a>, were hitched to wheeled carts by means of a <a href="/wiki/Yoke" title="Yoke">yoke</a> around their necks in a manner similar to that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxen" title="Oxen">oxen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard-54">[53]</a></sup> However, such a design is incompatible with <a href="/wiki/Equine_anatomy" title="Equine anatomy">equine anatomy</a>, limiting both the strength and mobility of the animal. By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> invasions of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, c. 1600 BC, horses were pulling chariots with an improved <a href="/wiki/Horse_harness" title="Horse harness">harness</a> design that made use of a <a href="/wiki/Breastplate_(tack)" title="Breastplate (tack)">breastcollar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breeching_(tack)" title="Breeching (tack)">breeching</a>, which allowed a horse to move faster and pull more weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin102_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin102-55">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Even after the chariot had become obsolete as a tool of war, there still was a need for technological innovations in pulling technologies; horses were needed to pull heavy loads of supplies and weapons. The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Horse_collar" title="Horse collar">horse collar</a> in China during the 5th century AD (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_and_Northern_Dynasties" title="Southern and Northern Dynasties">Southern and Northern Dynasties</a>) allowed horses to pull greater weight than they could when hitched to a vehicle with the ox yokes or breast collars used in earlier times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_volume_4_part_2_322_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_volume_4_part_2_322-56">[55]</a></sup> The horse collar arrived in Europe during the 9th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin109_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin109-57">[56]</a></sup> and became widespread by the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham317_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham317-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Riding_equipment">Riding equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Riding equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">Saddle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stirrup" title="Stirrup">Stirrup</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HaniwaHorse.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="511" data-file-width="520" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/HaniwaHorse.JPG/220px-HaniwaHorse.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/HaniwaHorse.JPG/330px-HaniwaHorse.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/HaniwaHorse.JPG/440px-HaniwaHorse.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HaniwaHorse.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Haniwa" title="Haniwa">Haniwa</a> horse statuette, complete with saddle and stirrups, 6th century, <a href="/wiki/Kofun_period" title="Kofun period">Kofun period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two major innovations that revolutionised the effectiveness of mounted warriors in battle were the saddle and the stirrup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Techniques70_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Techniques70-59">[58]</a></sup> Riders quickly learned to pad their horse's backs to protect themselves from the horse's spine and <a href="/wiki/Withers" title="Withers">withers</a>, and fought on horseback for centuries with little more than a blanket or pad on the horse's back and a rudimentary bridle. To help distribute the rider's weight and protect the horse's back, some cultures created stuffed padding that resembles the panels of today's <a href="/wiki/English_saddle" title="English saddle">English saddle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett43_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett43-60">[59]</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a> used pads with added felt attached with a <a href="/wiki/Surcingle" title="Surcingle">surcingle</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horse_tack" title="Horse tack">girth</a> around the horse's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horse_anatomy" title="Horse anatomy">barrel</a> for increased security and comfort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis14_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis14-61">[60]</a></sup> Xenophon mentioned the use of a padded cloth on cavalry mounts as early as the 4th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The saddle with a solid framework, or "<a href="/wiki/Saddle#Parts_of_an_equestrian_saddle" title="Saddle">tree</a>", provided a bearing surface to protect the horse from the weight of the rider, but was not widespread until the 2nd century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup> However, it made a critical difference, as horses could carry more weight when distributed across a solid saddle tree. A solid tree, the predecessor of today's <a href="/wiki/Western_saddle" title="Western saddle">Western saddle</a>, also allowed a more built-up seat to give the rider greater security in the saddle. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> are credited with the invention of the solid-treed saddle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Show_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Show-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>An invention that made cavalry particularly effective was the stirrup. A toe loop that held the big toe was used in India possibly as early as 500 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin110_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin110-63">[62]</a></sup> and later a single stirrup was used as a mounting aid. The first set of paired stirrups appeared in China about 322 AD during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_Dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin Dynasty (265–420)">Jin Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis51_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis51-65">[64]</a></sup> Following the invention of paired stirrups, which allowed a rider greater leverage with weapons, as well as both increased stability and mobility while mounted, nomadic groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_military_tactics_and_organization" title="Mongol military tactics and organization">Mongols</a> adopted this technology and developed a decisive military advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin110_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin110-63">[62]</a></sup> By the 7th century, due primarily to invaders from Central Asia, stirrup technology spread from Asia to Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dictionary300_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dictionary300-66">[65]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avar" title="Pannonian Avar">Avar</a> invaders are viewed as primarily responsible for spreading the use of the stirrup into central Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curta319_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curta319-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-68">[67]</a></sup> However, while stirrups were known in Europe in the 8th century, pictorial and literary references to their use date only from the 9th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warfare88_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warfare88-69">[68]</a></sup> Widespread use in Northern Europe, including England, is credited to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a>, who spread the stirrup in the 9th and 10th centuries to those areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warfare88_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warfare88-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seaby_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seaby-70">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christiansen_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christiansen-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics">Tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="963" data-file-width="2202" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg/220px-Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg/330px-Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg/440px-Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standard_of_Ur_-_War.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "War Panel" of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_Ur" title="Standard of Ur">Standard of Ur</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> evidence of horses used in warfare dates from between 4000 and 3000 BC in the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, in what today is <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>. Not long after <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domestication of the horse</a>, people in these locations began to live together in large fortified towns for protection from the threat of horseback-riding raiders,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Show_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Show-62">[61]</a></sup> who could attack and escape faster than people of more sedentary cultures could follow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN188_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN188-73">[72]</a></sup> Horse-mounted nomads of the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> and current day <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> spread <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European_Languages" title="Indo-European Languages">Indo-European Languages</a> as they conquered other tribes and groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of horses in organised warfare was also documented early in recorded history. One of the first depictions of equids is the "war panel" of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_Ur" title="Standard of Ur">Standard of Ur</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, dated c. 2500 BC, showing horses (or possibly onagers or mules) pulling a four-wheeled wagon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pritchard_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard-54">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chariot_warfare">Chariot warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Chariot warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">Chariot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chariot_tactics" title="Chariot tactics">Chariot tactics</a></div>
<p>Among the earliest evidence of chariot use are the burials of horse and chariot remains by the <a href="/wiki/Andronovo_culture" title="Andronovo culture">Andronovo</a> (Sintashta-Petrovka) culture in modern Russia and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, dated to approximately 2000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The oldest documentary evidence of what was probably chariot warfare in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a> is the Old Hittite <a href="/wiki/Anitta" title="Anitta">Anitta</a> text, of the 18th century BC, which mentioned 40 teams of horses at the siege of <a href="/wiki/Salatiwara" title="Salatiwara">Salatiwara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> became well known throughout the ancient world for their prowess with the chariot. Widespread use of the chariot in warfare across most of Eurasia coincides approximately with the development of the <a href="/wiki/Composite_bow" title="Composite bow">composite bow</a>, known from c. 1600 BC. Further improvements in wheels and axles, as well as innovations in weaponry, soon resulted in chariots being driven in battle by <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> societies from China to Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartwick_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hartwick-53">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> invaders brought the chariot to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> in the 16th century BC and the Egyptians adopted its use from that time forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> The oldest preserved text related to the handling of war horses in the ancient world is the Hittite manual of <a href="/wiki/Kikkuli" title="Kikkuli">Kikkuli</a>, which dates to about 1350 BC, and describes the conditioning of chariot horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin48_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin48-43">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Chariots existed in the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a>, as they were inventoried on storage lists from <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Life94_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Life94-81">[80]</a></sup> dating to around 1450 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willetts209_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willetts209-82">[81]</a></sup> Chariots were also used in China as far back as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Dynasty" title="Shang Dynasty">Shang Dynasty</a> (c. 1600–1050 BC), where they appear in burials. The high point of chariot use in China was in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> (770–476 BC), although they continued in use up until the 2nd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dictionary67_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dictionary67-83">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Descriptions of the tactical role of chariots in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a> are rare. The <a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a>, possibly referring to <a href="/wiki/Mycenae" title="Mycenae">Mycenaen</a> practices used c. 1250 BC, describes the use of chariots for transporting warriors to and from battle, rather than for actual fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Life94_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Life94-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, invading Britain in 55 and 54 BC, noted British charioteers throwing javelins, then leaving their chariots to fight on foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warry220_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warry220-86">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cavalry">Cavalry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cavalry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">Cavalry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cavalry_tactics" title="Cavalry tactics">Cavalry tactics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knight-Iran.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="522" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Knight-Iran.JPG/220px-Knight-Iran.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Knight-Iran.JPG/330px-Knight-Iran.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Knight-Iran.JPG/440px-Knight-Iran.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knight-Iran.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sasanian" title="Sasanian">Sasanian</a> Persian <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">Cataphract</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taq-e_Bostan" title="Taq-e Bostan">Taq-e Bostan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some of the earliest examples of horses being ridden in warfare were <a href="/wiki/Mounted_archery" title="Mounted archery">horse-mounted archers</a> or spear-throwers, dating to the reigns of the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> rulers <a href="/wiki/Ashurnasirpal_II" title="Ashurnasirpal II">Ashurnasirpal II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">Shalmaneser III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trench_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trench-51">[50]</a></sup> However, these riders sat far back on their horses, a precarious position for moving quickly, and the horses were held by a handler on the ground, keeping the archer free to use the bow. Thus, these archers were more a type of <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a> than true cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup> The Assyrians developed <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> in response to invasions by nomadic people from the north, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a>, who entered <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a> in the 8th century BC and took over parts of <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">Urartu</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">Sargon II</a>, approximately 721 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Mounted warriors such as the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> also had an influence on the region in the 7th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis14_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis14-61">[60]</a></sup> By the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">Ashurbanipal</a> in 669 BC, the Assyrians had learned to sit forward on their horses in the classic riding position still seen today and could be said to be true <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> used both light horse scouts and heavy cavalry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hope_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hope-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trench_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trench-51">[50]</a></sup> although not extensively, possibly due to the cost of keeping horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Life94_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Life94-81">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">Heavy cavalry</a> was believed to have been developed by the Ancient <a href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trench_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-Trench-51">[50]</a></sup> although others argue for the <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perevalov_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perevalov-88">[87]</a></sup> By the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I_of_Persia" title="Darius I of Persia">Darius</a> (558–486 BC), Persian military tactics required horses and riders that were completely armoured, and <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selectively bred</a> a heavier, more muscled horse to carry the additional weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards11ff_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards11ff-24">[24]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cataphract" title="Cataphract">cataphract</a> was a type of heavily armoured cavalry with distinct tactics, armour, and weaponry used from the time of the Persians up until the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Techniques76_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Techniques76-89">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phillip_of_Macedon" title="Phillip of Macedon">Phillip of Macedon</a> is credited with developing tactics allowing massed cavalry charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> The most famous Greek heavy cavalry units were the <a href="/wiki/Companion_cavalry" title="Companion cavalry">companion cavalry</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin154_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin154-91">[90]</a></sup> The Chinese of the 4th century BC during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (403–221 BC) began to use cavalry against rival states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ebrey_29_30_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ebrey_29_30-92">[91]</a></sup> To fight nomadic raiders from the north and west, the Chinese of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> (202 BC – 220 AD) developed effective mounted units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodrich32_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodrich32-93">[92]</a></sup> Cavalry was not used extensively by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> period, but by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, they made use of heavy cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis30_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis30-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RomeLife51_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-RomeLife51-95">[94]</a></sup> However, the backbone of the Roman army was the infantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horse_artillery">Horse artillery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Horse artillery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horse_artillery" title="Horse artillery">Horse artillery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horse_artillery_rear.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Horse_artillery_rear.jpg/220px-Horse_artillery_rear.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Horse_artillery_rear.jpg/330px-Horse_artillery_rear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Horse_artillery_rear.jpg/440px-Horse_artillery_rear.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horse_artillery_rear.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A lifesize model of a c. 1850 Swedish horse <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> team towing a light artillery piece</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="2814" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg/220px-Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg/330px-Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg/440px-Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sgt_Reckless_with_recoilless-rifle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sergeant_Reckless" title="Sergeant Reckless">Sergeant Reckless</a>, a highly decorated US Marine Corps artillery horse in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, pictured beside a 75 mm recoilless rifle in the 1950s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once gunpowder was invented, another major use of horses was as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draft_animal" title="Draft animal">draught animals</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_artillery" title="Heavy artillery">heavy artillery</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>. In addition to <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery" title="Field artillery">field artillery</a>, where horse-drawn guns were attended by gunners on foot, many armies had <a href="/wiki/Artillery_battery" title="Artillery battery">artillery batteries</a> where each gunner was provided with a mount.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi124_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi124-97">[96]</a></sup> Horse artillery units generally used lighter pieces, pulled by six horses. "9-pounders" were pulled by eight horses, and heavier artillery pieces needed a team of twelve. With the individual riding horses required for officers, surgeons and other support staff, as well as those pulling the artillery guns and supply wagons, an artillery battery of six guns could require 160 to 200 horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi130_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi130-98">[97]</a></sup> Horse artillery usually came under the command of cavalry divisions, but in some battles, such as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Waterloo</a>, the horse artillery were used as a rapid response force, repulsing attacks and assisting the infantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH415_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-MH415-99">[98]</a></sup> Agility was important; the ideal artillery horse was 15 to 16 hands high, strongly built, but able to move quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotner1_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cotner1-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_military_tactics_and_organization" title="Mongol military tactics and organization">Mongol military tactics and organization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nomadic_empire" title="Nomadic empire">Nomadic empire</a></div>
<p>Relations between <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> nomads and the settled people in and around <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> were often marked by conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarfareNeighbors185_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-WarfareNeighbors185-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis120_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis120-101">[100]</a></sup> The nomadic lifestyle was well suited to warfare, and steppe cavalry became some of the most militarily potent forces in the world, only limited by nomads' frequent lack of internal unity. Periodically, strong leaders would organise several tribes into one force, creating an almost unstoppable power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attila6_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Attila6-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> These unified groups included the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, who invaded Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Attila20_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Attila20-104">[103]</a></sup> and under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attila_the_Hun" title="Attila the Hun">Attila</a>, conducted campaigns in both eastern France and northern Italy, over 500 miles apart, within two successive campaign seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN188_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN188-73">[72]</a></sup> Other unified nomadic forces included the <a href="/wiki/Five_Barbarians" title="Five Barbarians">Wu Hu</a> attacks on China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodrich83_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodrich83-105">[104]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Mongol_conquests" title="Timeline of Mongol conquests">Mongol conquest</a> of much of Eurasia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WarfareNeighbors91_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-WarfareNeighbors91-106">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kurukshetra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="614" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg/350px-Kurukshetra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg/525px-Kurukshetra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/81/Kurukshetra.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kurukshetra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Manuscript illustration of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahabharata_War" title="Mahabharata War">Mahabharata War</a>, depicting warriors fighting on horse chariots</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_horse_in_South_Asia" title="History of the horse in South Asia">History of the horse in South Asia</a></div>
<p>The literature of ancient <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> describes numerous horse nomads. Some of the earliest references to the use of horses in South Asian warfare are <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranic</a> texts, which refer to an attempted invasion of India by the joint cavalry forces of the <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Sakas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yona" title="Yona">Yavanas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Pahlavas" title="The Pahlavas">Pahlavas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paradas" title="Paradas">Paradas</a>, called the "five hordes" (<i>pañca.ganah</i>) or "<a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">Kśatriya</a>" hordes (<i>Kśatriya ganah</i>). About 1600 BC, they captured the throne of <a href="/wiki/Ayodhya" title="Ayodhya">Ayodhya</a> by dethroning the <a href="/wiki/Vedic_period" title="Vedic period">Vedic</a> king, Bahu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partiger_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partiger-107">[106]</a></sup> Later texts, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81bh%C4%81rata" title="Mahābhārata">Mahābhārata</a>, c. 950 BC, appear to recognise efforts taken to <a href="/wiki/Horse_breeding" title="Horse breeding">breed</a> war horses and develop trained mounted warriors, stating that the horses of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Sindhu</a> and Kamboja regions were of the finest quality, and the Kambojas, Gandharas, and Yavanas were expert in fighting from horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In technological innovation, the early toe loop stirrup is credited to the cultures of India, and may have been in use as early as 500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chamberlin110_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chamberlin110-63">[62]</a></sup> Not long after, the cultures of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> clashed with those of central Asia and India. <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (484–425 BC) wrote that <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandarian</a> mercenaries of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> were recruited into the army of emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerxes_I_of_Persia" title="Xerxes I of Persia">Xerxes I of Persia</a> (486–465 BC), which he led against the Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> A century later, the "Men of the Mountain Land," from north of <a href="/wiki/Kabul_River" title="Kabul River">Kabul River</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[note 2]</a></sup> served in the army of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_III_of_Persia" title="Darius III of Persia">Darius III of Persia</a> when he fought against <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Arbela</a> in 331 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmstead232_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olmstead232-112">[111]</a></sup> In battle against Alexander at Massaga in 326 BC, the Assakenoi forces included 20,000 cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mudrarakshasa" title="Mudrarakshasa">Mudra-Rakshasa</a> recounted how cavalry of the Shakas, Yavanas, Kambojas, <a href="/wiki/Kirata" title="Kirata">Kiratas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Parasikas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bahlikas" title="Bahlikas">Bahlikas</a> helped <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> (c. 320–298 BC) defeat the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> and take the throne, thus laying the foundations of <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Mauryan Dynasty</a> in Northern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> cavalry used gunpowder weapons, but were slow to replace the traditional composite bow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon230_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon230-116">[114]</a></sup> Under the impact of European military successes in India, some Indian rulers adopted the European system of massed cavalry charges, although others did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon241_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon241-117">[115]</a></sup> By the 18th century, Indian armies continued to field cavalry, but mainly of the heavy variety.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Asia">East Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: East Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yabusame00.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="720" height="92" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Yabusame00.jpg/220px-Yabusame00.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Yabusame00.jpg/330px-Yabusame00.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Yabusame00.jpg/440px-Yabusame00.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yabusame00.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Yabusame archers, <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_East_Asian_warfare" title="Horses in East Asian warfare">Horses in East Asian warfare</a></div>
<p>The Chinese used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chariot_(Ancient_China)" title="Chariot (Ancient China)">chariots</a> for horse-based warfare until light cavalry forces became common during the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a> era (402–221 BC). A major proponent of the change to riding horses from chariots was <a href="/wiki/King_Wuling_of_Zhao" title="King Wuling of Zhao">Wu Ling</a>, c. 320 BC. However, conservative forces in China often opposed change, and cavalry never became as dominant as in Europe. Cavalry in China also did not benefit from the additional cachet attached to being the military branch dominated by the nobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis19_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis19-118">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Japanese <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> fought as cavalry for many centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turnbull15_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turnbull15-119">[117]</a></sup> They were particularly skilled in the art of using archery from horseback. The archery skills of mounted samurai were developed by training such as <a href="/wiki/Yabusame" title="Yabusame">Yabusame</a>, which originated in 530 AD and reached its peak under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minamoto_Yoritomo" title="Minamoto Yoritomo">Minamoto Yoritomo</a> (1147–1199 AD) in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamakura_Period" title="Kamakura Period">Kamakura Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> They switched from an emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Mounted_archery" title="Mounted archery">mounted bowmen</a> to mounted spearmen during the <a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku period</a> (1467–1615 AD).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Furusiyya" title="Furusiyya">Furusiyya</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Higueruela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="2135" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Higueruela.jpg/170px-Higueruela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Higueruela.jpg/255px-Higueruela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Higueruela.jpg/340px-Higueruela.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Higueruela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spanish and Moorish light cavalry (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jinetes" title="Jinetes">jinetes</a>) skirmish at the 1431 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_La_Higueruela" title="Battle of La Higueruela">Battle of La Higueruela</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the period when various <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Islamic empires</a> controlled much of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> as well as parts of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> armies consisted mostly of cavalry, made up of fighters from various local groups, <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" title="Turkmen people">Turkoman</a> tribesmen. The latter were considered particularly skilled as both lancers and mounted archers. In the 9th century the use of <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a>, slaves raised to be soldiers for various Muslim rulers, became increasingly common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> Mobile tactics, advanced <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">breeding</a> of horses, and detailed training manuals made Mamluk cavalry a highly efficient fighting force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> The use of armies consisting mostly of cavalry continued among the Turkish people who founded the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. Their need for large mounted forces lead to an establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Sipahi" title="Sipahi">sipahi</a>, cavalry soldiers who were granted lands in exchange for providing <a href="/wiki/Military_service" title="Military service">military service</a> in times of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mounted Muslim warriors conquered North Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> during the 7th and 8th centuries AD following the Hegira, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">Hijra</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> in 622 AD. By 630 AD, their influence expanded across the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and into western <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>. By 711 AD, the light cavalry of Muslim warriors had reached Spain, and controlled most of the Iberian peninsula by 720.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> Their mounts were of various oriental types, including the North African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barb_(horse)" title="Barb (horse)">Barb</a>. A few <a href="/wiki/Arabian_horse" title="Arabian horse">Arabian horses</a> may have come with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ummayad" title="Ummayad">Ummayads</a> who settled in the <a href="/wiki/Guadalquivir" title="Guadalquivir">Guadalquivir</a> valley. Another strain of horse that came with Islamic invaders was the <a href="/wiki/Turkoman_horse" title="Turkoman horse">Turkoman horse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland55_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland55-125">[123]</a></sup> Muslim invaders travelled north from nowadays Spain into France, where they were defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Tours</a> in 732 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hippeis" title="Hippeis">Hippeis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Companion_cavalry" title="Companion cavalry">Companion cavalry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auxilia" title="Auxilia">Auxilia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_cavalry" title="Roman cavalry">Roman cavalry</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_warfare" title="Celtic warfare">Celtic warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_warfare" title="Ancient warfare">Ancient warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_army" title="Ancient Macedonian army">Ancient Macedonian army</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_armies" title="Hellenistic armies">Hellenistic armies</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Middle_Ages">The Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Horses in the Middle Ages">Horses in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
