<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BNF_6465,_fol._154v.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="600" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg/170px-BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg/255px-BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg/340px-BNF_6465%2C_fol._154v.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BNF_6465,_fol._154v.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A re-imagination of <a href="/wiki/Louis_III_of_France" title="Louis III of France">Louis III</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carloman_II_of_France" title="Carloman II of France">Carloman</a>'s 879 victory over the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">vikings</a>; <a href="/wiki/Jean_Fouquet" title="Jean Fouquet">Jean Fouquet</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Grandes_Chroniques_de_France" title="Grandes Chroniques de France">Grandes Chroniques de France</a></i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the European <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, there were three primary types of war horses: The <a href="/wiki/Destrier" title="Destrier">destrier</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Courser_(horse)" title="Courser (horse)">courser</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rouncey" title="Rouncey">rouncey</a>, which differed in size and usage. A generic word used to describe medieval war horses was <i>charger,</i> which appears interchangeable with the other terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prestwich347_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prestwich347-127">[125]</a></sup> The medieval war horse was of moderate size, rarely exceeding 15.2 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (62 inches, 157 cm). Heavy horses were logistically difficult to maintain and less adaptable to varied terrains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH416_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-MH416-128">[126]</a></sup> The destrier of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early Middle Ages</a> was moderately larger than the courser or rouncey, in part to accommodate heavier armoured <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France23_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-France23-129">[127]</a></sup> However, destriers were not as large as <a href="/wiki/Draft_horse" title="Draft horse">draught horses</a>, averaging between 14.2 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (58 inches, 147 cm) and 15 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (60 inches, 152 cm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland85_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland85-26">[26]</a></sup> On the European continent, the need to carry more armour against mounted enemies such as the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frisians" title="Frisians">Frisians</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> developing heavier, bigger horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis47_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis47-130">[128]</a></sup> As the amount of armour and equipment increased in the later Middle Ages, the height of the horses increased; some late medieval horse skeletons were of horses over 15 hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France23_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-France23-129">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stallion_(horse)" title="Stallion (horse)">Stallions</a> were often used as destriers due to their natural aggression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bumke_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bumke-131">[129]</a></sup> However, there may have been some use of <a href="/wiki/Mare" title="Mare">mares</a> by European warriors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bumke_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bumke-131">[129]</a></sup> and mares, who were quieter and less likely to call out and betray their position to the enemy, were the preferred war horse of the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, who invaded various parts of Southern Europe from 700 AD through the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gelding" title="Gelding">Geldings</a> were used in war by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a>, and known as "monk horses" (German <i>Mönchpferde</i> or <i>Mönchhengste</i>). One advantage was if captured by the enemy, they could not be used to improve local bloodstock, thus maintaining the Knights' superiority in horseflesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The heavy cavalry charge, while it could be effective, was not a common occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Battles were rarely fought on land suitable for heavy cavalry. While mounted riders remained effective for initial attacks,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> by the end of the 14th century, it was common for knights to dismount to fight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> while their horses were sent to the rear, kept ready for pursuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler32_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler32-137">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pitched_battle" title="Pitched battle">Pitched battles</a> were avoided if possible, with most offensive <a href="/wiki/Medieval_warfare" title="Medieval warfare">warfare</a> in the early Middle Ages taking the form of <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">sieges</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> and in the later Middle Ages as mounted raids called <i><a href="/wiki/Chevauch%C3%A9e" title="Chevauchée">chevauchées</a></i>, with lightly armed warriors on swift horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[note 3]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jousting_renfair.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="2163" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jousting_renfair.jpg/220px-Jousting_renfair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jousting_renfair.jpg/330px-Jousting_renfair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jousting_renfair.jpg/440px-Jousting_renfair.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jousting_renfair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jousting" title="Jousting">Jousting</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Hastilude" title="Hastilude">sport</a> that evolved out of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a> practice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The war horse was also seen in <a href="/wiki/Hastilude" title="Hastilude">hastiludes</a>—martial war games such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joust" title="Joust">joust</a>, which began in the 11th century both as sport and to provide training for battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[139]</a></sup> Specialised destriers were bred for the purpose,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyland88_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyland88-143">[140]</a></sup> although the expense of keeping, training, and outfitting them kept the majority of the population from owning one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis43_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis43-144">[141]</a></sup> While some historians suggest that the tournament had become a theatrical event by the 15th and 16th centuries, others argue that <a href="/wiki/Jousting" title="Jousting">jousting</a> continued to help cavalry train for battle until the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tournaments_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tournaments-145">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition">Transition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Transition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The decline of the armoured knight was probably linked to changing structures of armies and various economic factors, and not obsolescence due to new technologies. However, some historians attribute the demise of the knight to the invention of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale54_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hale54-146">[143]</a></sup> or to the <a href="/wiki/English_longbow" title="English longbow">English longbow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis65_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis65-147">[144]</a></sup> Some link the decline to both technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Techniques123_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Techniques123-148">[145]</a></sup> Others argue these technologies actually contributed to the development of knights: <a href="/wiki/Plate_armour" title="Plate armour">plate armour</a> was first developed to resist early medieval <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> bolts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-149">[146]</a></sup> and the full harness worn by the early 15th century developed to resist longbow arrows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carey_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-150">[147]</a></sup> From the 14th century on, most plate was made from hardened steel, which resisted early musket ammunition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-149">[146]</a></sup> In addition, stronger designs did not make plate heavier; a full harness of musket-proof plate from the 17th century weighed 70 pounds (32 kg), significantly less than 16th century tournament armour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The move to predominately infantry-based battles from 1300 to 1550 was linked to both improved <a href="/wiki/Infantry_tactics" title="Infantry tactics">infantry tactics</a> and changes in <a href="/wiki/List_of_medieval_weapons" title="List of medieval weapons">weaponry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup> By the 16th century, the concept of a combined-arms professional army had spread throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carey_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carey-150">[147]</a></sup> Professional armies emphasized training, and were paid via contracts, a change from the ransom and pillaging which reimbursed knights in the past. When coupled with the rising costs involved in outfitting and maintaining armour and horses, the traditional knightly classes began to abandon their profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup> Light horses, or <i>prickers</i>, were still used for scouting and reconnaissance; they also provided a defensive screen for marching armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadler32_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sadler32-137">[135]</a></sup> Large teams of draught horses or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxen" title="Oxen">oxen</a> pulled the heavy early <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[151]</a></sup> Other horses pulled wagons and carried supplies for the armies.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_period">Early modern period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Early modern period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a> the shift continued from heavy cavalry and the armoured knight to unarmoured <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussars" title="Hussars">Hussars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_cheval" title="Chasseurs à cheval">Chasseurs à cheval</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowley_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowley-155">[152]</a></sup> Light cavalry facilitated better communication, using fast, agile horses to move quickly across battlefields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis98_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis98-156">[153]</a></sup> The ratio of footmen to horsemen also increased over the period as infantry weapons improved and footmen became more mobile and versatile, particularly once the musket <a href="/wiki/Bayonet" title="Bayonet">bayonet</a> replaced the more cumbersome pike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a>, mounted units included <a href="/wiki/Cuirassier" title="Cuirassier">cuirassiers</a>, heavily armoured and equipped with lances; light cavalry, who wore mail and bore light lances and pistols; and "<a href="/wiki/Petronel" title="Petronel">petronels</a>", who carried an early <a href="/wiki/Carbine" title="Carbine">carbine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ages32_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ages32-158">[155]</a></sup> As heavy cavalry use declined armour was increasingly abandoned and <a href="/wiki/Dragoon" title="Dragoon">dragoons</a>, whose horses were rarely used in combat, became more common: <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a> provided reconnaissance, escort and security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ages32_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ages32-158">[155]</a></sup> However, many generals still used the heavy mounted charge, from the late 17th century and early 18th century, where sword-wielding wedge-formation shock troops penetrated enemy lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup> to the early 19th century, where armoured heavy cuirassiers were employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH188_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-MH188-160">[157]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedland,_1807_(1875)_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1102" data-file-width="2000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg/400px-Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg/600px-Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg/800px-Friedland%2C_1807_%281875%29_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedland,_1807_(1875)_Ernest_Meissonier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chasseurs_%C3%A0_Cheval_de_la_Garde_Imp%C3%A9riale" title="Chasseurs à Cheval de la Garde Impériale">Chasseurs of the Guard</a> (<a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a>) to the left and <a href="/wiki/Cuirassier" title="Cuirassier">cuirassier</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">Heavy cavalry</a>) to the right, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Friedland" title="Battle of Friedland">battle of Friedland</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Light cavalry continued to play a major role, particularly after the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a> when Hussars started to play a larger part in battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hollins_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hollins-161">[158]</a></sup> Though some leaders preferred tall horses for their mounted troops this was as much for prestige as for increased shock ability and many troops used more typical horses, averaging 15 hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH416_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-MH416-128">[126]</a></sup> Cavalry tactics altered with fewer mounted charges, more reliance on drilled manoeuvres at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trot_(horse_gait)" title="Trot (horse gait)">trot</a>, and use of firearms once within range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> Ever-more elaborate movements, such as wheeling and <a href="/wiki/Caracole" title="Caracole">caracole</a>, were developed to facilitate the use of firearms from horseback. These tactics were not greatly successful in battle since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pikemen" title="Pikemen">pikemen</a> protected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musketeers" title="Musketeers">musketeers</a> could deny cavalry room to manoeuvre. However the advanced <a href="/wiki/Equestrianism" title="Equestrianism">equestrianism</a> required survives into the modern world as <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">dressage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keegan341_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keegan341-164">[161]</a></sup> While restricted, cavalry was not rendered obsolete. As infantry formations developed in tactics and skills, artillery became essential to break formations; in turn, cavalry was required to both combat enemy artillery, which was susceptible to cavalry while deploying, and to charge enemy infantry formations broken by artillery fire. Thus, successful warfare depended in a balance of the three arms: cavalry, artillery and infantry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>As regimental structures developed many units selected horses of uniform type and some, such as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots_Greys" title="Royal Scots Greys">Royal Scots Greys</a>, even specified colour. Trumpeters often rode distinctive horses so they stood out. Regional armies developed type preferences, such as British <a href="/wiki/Field_hunter" title="Field hunter">hunters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanoverian_(horse)" title="Hanoverian (horse)">Hanoverians</a> in central Europe, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Don" title="Russian Don">steppe ponies</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cossack" title="Cossack">Cossacks</a>, but once in the field, the lack of supplies typical of wartime meant that horses of all types were used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH417_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-MH417-166">[163]</a></sup> Since horses were such a vital component of most armies in early modern Europe, many instituted <a href="/wiki/Stud_farm#State_stud_farms" title="Stud farm">state stud farms</a> to breed horses for the military. However, in wartime, supply rarely matched the demand, resulting in some cavalry troops fighting on foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH416_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-MH416-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_the_Napoleonic_Wars" title="Horses in the Napoleonic Wars">Horses in the Napoleonic Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="587" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg/330px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg/440px-Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Elsholtz_Napoleon_I._mit_seinen_Generalen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon I</a> with his Generals" by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Elsholtz" title="Ludwig Elsholtz">Ludwig Elsholtz</a>. This painting shows light cavalry horses which come into use as officer's mounts in 18th- and 19th-century Europe.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 19th century distinctions between heavy and light cavalry became less significant; by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_War" title="Peninsular War">Peninsular War</a>, heavy cavalry were performing the scouting and outpost duties previously undertaken by light cavalry, and by the end of the 19th century the roles had effectively merged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colonial25_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colonial25-167">[164]</a></sup> Most armies at the time preferred cavalry horses to stand 15.2 <a href="/wiki/Hand_(unit)" title="Hand (unit)">hands</a> (62 inches, 157 cm) and weigh 990 to 1,100 pounds (450 to 500 kg), although <a href="/wiki/Cuirassier" title="Cuirassier">cuirassiers</a> frequently had heavier horses. Lighter horses were used for scouting and raiding. Cavalry horses were generally obtained at 5 years of age and were in service from 10 or 12 years, barring loss. However losses of 30–40% were common during a campaign due to conditions of the march as well as enemy action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi109_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi109-168">[165]</a></sup> Mares and <a href="/wiki/Gelding" title="Gelding">geldings</a> were preferred over less-easily managed stallions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi108_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi108-169">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> the cavalry's main offensive role was as shock troops. In defence cavalry were used to attack and harass the enemy's infantry flanks as they advanced. Cavalry were frequently used prior to an infantry assault, to force an infantry line to break and reform into formations vulnerable to infantry or artillery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi204_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi204-170">[167]</a></sup> Infantry frequently followed behind in order to secure any ground won<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi1756_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi1756-171">[168]</a></sup> or the cavalry could be used to break up enemy lines following a successful infantry action.</p>
<p>Mounted charges were carefully managed. A charge's maximum speed was 20 km/h; moving faster resulted in a break in formation and fatigued horses. Charges occurred across clear rising ground, and were effective against infantry both on the march and when deployed in a <a href="/wiki/Line_(formation)" title="Line (formation)">line</a> or <a href="/wiki/Column_(formation)" title="Column (formation)">column</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nofi176_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi176-172">[169]</a></sup> A foot <a href="/wiki/Battalion" title="Battalion">battalion</a> formed in line was vulnerable to cavalry, and could be broken or destroyed by a well-formed charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hay12_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hay12-173">[170]</a></sup> Traditional cavalry functions altered by the end of the 19th century. Many cavalry units transferred in title and role to "mounted rifles": troops trained to fight on foot, but retaining mounts for rapid deployment, as well as for patrols, scouting, communications, and defensive screening. These troops differed from <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a>, who used horses for transport but did not perform the old cavalry roles of reconnaissance and support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="760" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg/220px-Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg/330px-Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg/440px-Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Kanem-Bu_warriors.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kanem-Bu warriors armed with spears. <i>The Earth and Its Inhabitants</i>, 1892.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Horses were used for warfare in the central <a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">Sudan</a> since the 9th century, where they were considered "the most precious commodity following the slave."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup> The first conclusive evidence of horses playing a major role in the warfare of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> dates to the 11th century when the region was controlled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravid" title="Almoravid">Almoravids</a>, a Muslim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup> During the 13th and 14th centuries, cavalry became an important factor in the area. This coincided with the introduction of larger breeds of horse and the widespread adoption of saddles and stirrups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup> Increased mobility played a part in the formation of new power centers, such as the <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo Empire</a> in what today is <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>. The authority of many African Islamic states such as the <a href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> also rested in large part on their ability to subject neighboring peoples with cavalry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> Despite harsh climate conditions, endemic diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Trypanosomiasis" title="Trypanosomiasis">trypanosomiasis</a> the <a href="/wiki/African_horse_sickness" title="African horse sickness">African horse sickness</a> and unsuitable terrain that limited the effectiveness of horses in many parts of Africa, horses were continuously imported and were, in some areas, a vital instrument of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup> The introduction of horses also intensified existing conflicts, such as those between the <a href="/wiki/Herero_people" title="Herero people">Herero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nama_people" title="Nama people">Nama people</a> in Namibia during the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>The African slave trade was closely tied to the imports of war horses, and as the prevalence of slaving decreased, fewer horses were needed for raiding. This significantly decreased the amount of mounted warfare seen in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Law176_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Law176-181">[178]</a></sup> By the time of the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a> and the introduction of modern firearms in the 1880s, the use of horses in African warfare had lost most of its effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Law176_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-Law176-181">[178]</a></sup> Nonetheless, in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> (1899–1902), cavalry and other mounted troops were the major combat force for the British, since the horse-mounted Boers moved too quickly for infantry to engage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kinloch20_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kinloch20-182">[179]</a></sup> The Boers presented a mobile and innovative approach to warfare, drawing on strategies that had first appeared in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup> The terrain was not well-suited to the British horses, resulting in the loss of over 300,000 animals. As the campaign wore on, losses were replaced by more durable African <a href="/wiki/Basuto_pony" title="Basuto pony">Basuto ponies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Waler_horse" title="Waler horse">Waler horses</a> from <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH416_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-MH416-128">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Americas">The Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: The Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">Conquistador</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Wars" title="American Indian Wars">American Indian Wars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cavalry_(United_States)" title="Cavalry (United States)">Cavalry (United States)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cavalry_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Cavalry in the American Civil War">Cavalry in the American Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="362" data-file-width="609" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg/220px-Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg/330px-Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg/440px-Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comanche_Osage_fight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> quickly adopted the horse and were highly effective light cavalry. <i>Comanche-Osage fight.</i> <a href="/wiki/George_Catlin" title="George Catlin">George Catlin</a>, 1834</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The horse had been extinct in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a> for approximately 10,000 years prior to the arrival of Spanish <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">Conquistadors</a> in the early 16th century. Consequently, the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> had no warfare technologies that could overcome the considerable advantage provided by European horses and gunpowder weapons. In particular this resulted in the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca</a> empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup> The speed and increased impact of cavalry contributed to a number of early victories by European fighters in open terrain, though their success was limited in more mountainous regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker143_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker143-185">[182]</a></sup> The Incas' well-maintained roads in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> enabled quick mounted raids, such as those undertaken by the Spanish while resisting the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Cuzco" title="Siege of Cuzco">siege of Cuzco</a> in 1536–7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker143_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Parker143-185">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indigenous populations of South America soon learned to use horses. In Chile, the <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche</a> began using cavalry in the <a href="/wiki/Arauco_War" title="Arauco War">Arauco War</a> in 1586. They drove the Spanish out of <a href="/wiki/Araucan%C3%ADa_(historic_region)" title="Araucanía (historic region)">Araucanía</a> at the beginning of the 17th century. Later, the Mapuche conducted mounted raids known as <a href="/wiki/Mal%C3%B3n" title="Malón">Malónes</a>, first on Spanish, then on Chilean and Argentine settlements until well into the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup> In North America, <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> also quickly learned to use horses. In particular, the people of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Comanche" title="Comanche">Comanche</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cheyenne" title="Cheyenne">Cheyenne</a>, became renowned horseback fighters. By the 19th century, they presented a formidable force against the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis156_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis156-187">[184]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1357" data-file-width="1883" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg/220px-General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg/330px-General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg/440px-General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:General_R._E._Lee_and_Traveler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Confederate general <a href="/wiki/Robert_E._Lee" title="Robert E. Lee">Robert E. Lee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traveller_(horse)" title="Traveller (horse)">Traveller</a>. Cavalry played a significant role in the American Civil War.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> (1775–1783), the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> made relatively little use of cavalry, primarily relying on infantry and a few dragoon regiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[185]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> eventually authorized regiments specifically designated as cavalry in 1855. The newly formed American cavalry adopted tactics based on experiences fighting over vast distances during the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican War</a> (1846–1848) and against indigenous peoples on the western frontier, abandoning some European traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSU_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSU-189">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–1865), cavalry held the most important and respected role it would ever hold in the American military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSU_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-OSU-189">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[note 4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Field_artillery_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Field artillery in the American Civil War">Field artillery in the American Civil War</a> was also highly mobile. Both horses and mules pulled the guns, though only horses were used on the battlefield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cotner1_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cotner1-8">[8]</a></sup> At the beginning of the war, most of the experienced cavalry officers were from the South and thus joined the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>, leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_Army" title="Confederate Army">Confederate Army</a>'s initial battlefield superiority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSU_189-2"><a href="#cite_note-OSU-189">[186]</a></sup> The tide turned at the 1863 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Brandy_Station" title="Battle of Brandy Station">Battle of Brandy Station</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Gettysburg</a> campaign, where the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union cavalry</a>, in the largest cavalry battle ever fought on the North American continent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[note 5]</a></sup> ended the dominance of the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandy_Station_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brandy_Station-192">[188]</a></sup> By 1865, Union cavalry were decisive in achieving victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSU_189-3"><a href="#cite_note-OSU-189">[186]</a></sup> So important were <a href="/wiki/List_of_horses_of_the_American_Civil_War" title="List of horses of the American Civil War">horses to individual soldiers</a> that the surrender terms at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Appomattox_Courthouse" title="Battle of Appomattox Courthouse">Appomattox</a> allowed every Confederate cavalryman to take his horse home with him. This was because, unlike their Union counterparts, Confederate cavalrymen provided their own horses for service instead of drawing them from the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although cavalry was used extensively throughout the world during the 19th century, horses became less important to warfare after the beginning of the 20th century. <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">Light cavalry</a> was still seen on the battlefield at the beginning of the 20th century, but formal mounted cavalry began to be phased out for combat during and immediately after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, although units that included horses still had military uses well into <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waller_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waller-195">[190]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_horse_walers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1325" data-file-width="1200" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Light_horse_walers.jpg/170px-Light_horse_walers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Light_horse_walers.jpg/255px-Light_horse_walers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Light_horse_walers.jpg/340px-Light_horse_walers.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_horse_walers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/First_Australian_Imperial_Force" title="First Australian Imperial Force">Australian Imperial Force</a> light horsemen, 1914</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_World_War_I" title="Horses in World War I">Horses in World War I</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> saw great changes in the use of cavalry. The mode of warfare changed, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trench warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbed_wire" title="Barbed wire">barbed wire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine guns</a> rendered traditional cavalry almost obsolete. <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">Tanks</a>, introduced in 1917, began to take over the role of shock combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carver123_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carver123-196">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early in the War, cavalry skirmishes were common, and horse-mounted troops widely used for reconnaissance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILL46_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-WILL46-197">[192]</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> cavalry were an effective flanking force during the "<a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_Sea" title="Race to the Sea">Race to the Sea</a>" in 1914, but were less useful once <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trench warfare</a> was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILL60_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-WILL60-198">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILL99_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-WILL99-199">[194]</a></sup> There a few examples of successful shock combat, and cavalry divisions also provided important mobile firepower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH188_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-MH188-160">[157]</a></sup> Cavalry played a greater role on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Eastern Front (World War I)">Eastern Front</a>, where trench warfare was less common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WILL99_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-WILL99-199">[194]</a></sup> On the Eastern Front, and also against the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, the "cavalry was literally indispensable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH188_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-MH188-160">[157]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> cavalry proved adaptable, since they were trained to fight both on foot and while mounted, while other European cavalry relied primarily on shock action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH188_160-3"><a href="#cite_note-MH188-160">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>On both fronts, the horse was also used as a <a href="/wiki/Packhorse" title="Packhorse">pack animal</a>. Because railway lines could not withstand artillery bombardments, horses carried ammunition and supplies between the railheads and the rear trenches, though the horses generally were not used in the actual trench zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN308_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN308-200">[195]</a></sup> This role of horses was critical, and thus horse <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> was the single largest commodity shipped to the front by some countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN308_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN308-200">[195]</a></sup> Following the war, many cavalry regiments were converted to mechanised, armoured divisions, with light tanks developed to perform many of the cavalry's original roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[196]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PolishCavalryAttack.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="611" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PolishCavalryAttack.jpg/220px-PolishCavalryAttack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PolishCavalryAttack.jpg/330px-PolishCavalryAttack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/PolishCavalryAttack.jpg/440px-PolishCavalryAttack.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PolishCavalryAttack.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Polish Cavalry during a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_Army" title="Polish Army">Polish Army</a> manoeuvre in late 1930s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horses_in_World_War_II" title="Horses in World War II">Horses in World War II</a></div>
<p>Several nations used horse units during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> army used cavalry to defend against the armies of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland_(1939)" title="Invasion of Poland (1939)">1939 invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies324_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies324-202">[197]</a></sup> Both the Germans and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Soviet Union</a> maintained cavalry units throughout the war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH417_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-MH417-166">[163]</a></sup> particularly on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH188_160-4"><a href="#cite_note-MH188-160">[157]</a></sup> The British Army used horses early in the war, and the final British cavalry charge was on March 21, 1942, when the Burma Frontier Force encountered <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> infantry in central <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup> The only American cavalry unit during World War II was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/26th_Cavalry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="26th Cavalry Regiment (United States)">26th Cavalry</a>. They challenged the Japanese invaders of <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a>, holding off armoured and infantry regiments during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Philippines_(1941%E2%80%9342)" title="Battle of the Philippines (1941–42)">invasion of the Philippines</a>, repelled a unit of tanks in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binalonan" title="Binalonan">Binalonan</a>, and successfully held ground for the Allied armies' retreat to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bataan" title="Battle of Bataan">Bataan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the war, horses and mules were an essential form of transport, especially by the British in the rough terrain of Italy and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[200]</a></sup> The United States Army utilised a few cavalry and supply units during the war, but there were concerns that the Americans did not use horses often enough. In the campaigns in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, generals such as <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a> lamented their lack, saying, "had we possessed an American cavalry division with pack artillery in Tunisia and in Sicily, not a German would have escaped."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waller_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-Waller-195">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>The German and the Soviet armies used horses until the end of the war for transportation of troops and supplies. The German Army, strapped for motorised transport because its factories were needed to produce tanks and aircraft, used around 2.75 million horses—more than it had used in World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN308_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN308-200">[195]</a></sup> One German infantry division in Normandy in 1944 had 5,000 horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH417_166-2"><a href="#cite_note-MH417-166">[163]</a></sup> The Soviets used 3.5 million horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HISTORYKEEGAN308_200-3"><a href="#cite_note-HISTORYKEEGAN308-200">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Horse_Memorial-002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Horse_Memorial-002.jpg/220px-Horse_Memorial-002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Horse_Memorial-002.jpg/330px-Horse_Memorial-002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Horse_Memorial-002.jpg/440px-Horse_Memorial-002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Horse_Memorial-002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A memorial to the horses that served in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While many statues and memorials have been erected to human heroes of war, often shown with horses, a few have also been created specifically to honor horses or animals in general. One example is the <a href="/wiki/Horse_Memorial" title="Horse Memorial">Horse Memorial</a> in <a href="/wiki/Port_Elizabeth" title="Port Elizabeth">Port Elizabeth</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Cape" title="Eastern Cape">Eastern Cape</a> province of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahra_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-sahra-206">[201]</a></sup> Both horses and mules are honored in the <a href="/wiki/Animals_in_War_Memorial" title="Animals in War Memorial">Animals in War Memorial</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Park,_London" title="Hyde Park, London">London's Hyde Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-207">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>Horses have also at times received medals for extraordinary deeds. After the <a href="/wiki/Charge_of_the_Light_Brigade" title="Charge of the Light Brigade">Charge of the Light Brigade</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, a surviving horse named Drummer Boy, ridden by an officer of the <a href="/wiki/8th_King%27s_Royal_Irish_Hussars" title="8th King's Royal Irish Hussars">8th Hussars</a>, was given an unofficial campaign medal by his rider that was identical to those awarded to British troops who served in the Crimea, engraved with the horse's name and an inscription of his service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telegraph_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-208">[203]</a></sup> A more formal award was the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Dispensary_for_Sick_Animals" title="People's Dispensary for Sick Animals">PDSA</a> <a href="/wiki/Dickin_Medal" title="Dickin Medal">Dickin Medal</a>, an animals' equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a>, awarded by the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Dispensary_for_Sick_Animals" title="People's Dispensary for Sick Animals">People's Dispensary for Sick Animals</a> charity in the United Kingdom to three horses that served in World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-207">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_uses">Modern uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Modern uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Special_Forces_on_horseback,_Afghanistan,_2001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="518" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg/220px-US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg/330px-US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg/440px-US_Special_Forces_on_horseback%2C_Afghanistan%2C_2001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Special_Forces_on_horseback,_Afghanistan,_2001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Afghan and United States military forces on horseback in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, 2001</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Today, many of the historical military uses of the horse have evolved into peacetime applications, including exhibitions, <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical reenactments</a>, work of <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_officer" title="Law enforcement officer">peace officers</a>, and competitive events. Formal combat units of mounted cavalry are mostly a thing of the past, with horseback units within the modern military used for reconnaissance, ceremonial, or crowd control purposes. With the rise of mechanised technology, horses in formal national militias were displaced by <a href="/wiki/Tank" title="Tank">tanks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armored_fighting_vehicle" title="Armored fighting vehicle">armored fighting vehicles</a>, sometimes still referred to as "cavalry".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_military">Active military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Active military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Organised armed fighters on horseback are occasionally seen. The best-known current examples are the <a href="/wiki/Janjaweed" title="Janjaweed">Janjaweed</a>, militia groups seen in the <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, who became notorious for their attacks upon unarmed civilian populations in the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Darfur" title="War in Darfur">Darfur conflict</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[205]</a></sup> Many nations still maintain small numbers of mounted military units for certain types of patrol and reconnaissance duties in extremely rugged terrain, including the conflict in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Enduring_Freedom" title="Operation Enduring Freedom">Operation Enduring Freedom</a>, <a href="/wiki/5th_Special_Forces_Group_(United_States)#Fighting_on_horseback" title="5th Special Forces Group (United States)">Operational Detachment Alpha 595</a> teams were covertly inserted into Afghanistan on October 19, 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-briscoe_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-briscoe-212">[207]</a></sup> Horses were the only suitable transportation for the difficult mountainous terrain of Northern Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bissell_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-bissell-213">[208]</a></sup> They were the first U.S. soldiers to ride horses into battle since January 16, 1942, when the U.S. Army’s <a href="/wiki/26th_Cavalry_Regiment" title="26th Cavalry Regiment">26th Cavalry Regiment</a> charged an advanced guard of the 14th Japanese Army as it advanced from Manila.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Independent_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Independent-215">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>The only remaining operationally ready, fully horse-mounted regular <a href="/wiki/Regiment" title="Regiment">regiment</a> in the world is the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Army" title="Indian Army">Indian Army</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/61st_Cavalry_Regiment_(India)" title="61st Cavalry Regiment (India)">61st Cavalry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_enforcement_and_public_safety">Law enforcement and public safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Law enforcement and public safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="783" data-file-width="619" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/170px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/255px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg/340px-Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Policja_konna_Pozna%C5%84.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mounted police in <a href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84" title="Poznań">Poznań</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mounted_police" title="Mounted police">Mounted police</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mounted_search_and_rescue" title="Mounted search and rescue">Mounted search and rescue</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mounted_police" title="Mounted police">Mounted police</a> have been used since the 18th century, and still are used worldwide to control traffic and crowds, patrol public parks, keep order in processionals and during ceremonies and perform general street patrol duties. Today, many cities still have mounted police units. In rural areas, horses are used by law enforcement for mounted patrols over rugged terrain, crowd control at religious shrines, and border patrol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>In rural areas, law enforcement that operates outside of incorporated cities may also have mounted units. These include specially deputised, paid or volunteer <a href="/wiki/Mounted_search_and_rescue" title="Mounted search and rescue">mounted search and rescue</a> units sent into roadless areas on horseback to locate missing people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[214]</a></sup> Law enforcement in <a href="/wiki/Protected_area" title="Protected area">protected areas</a> may use horses in places where mechanised transport is difficult or prohibited. Horses can be an essential part of an overall team effort as they can move faster on the ground than a human on foot, can transport heavy equipment, and provide a more rested rescue worker when a subject is found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonial_and_educational_uses">Ceremonial and educational uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ceremonial and educational uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">Historical reenactment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charge3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="246" data-file-width="495" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Charge3.jpg/220px-Charge3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Charge3.jpg/330px-Charge3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Charge3.jpg/440px-Charge3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charge3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Horse Cavalry Detachment of the U.S. Army's 1st Cavalry Division demonstrating a mock cavalry charge at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Bliss,_Texas" title="Fort Bliss, Texas">Fort Bliss, Texas</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many countries throughout the world maintain traditionally trained and historically uniformed cavalry units for ceremonial, exhibition, or educational purposes. One example is the Horse Cavalry Detachment of the U.S. Army's <a href="/wiki/1st_Cavalry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Cavalry Division (United States)">1st Cavalry Division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[216]</a></sup> This unit of active duty soldiers approximates the weapons, tools, equipment and techniques used by the United States Cavalry in the 1880s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbell_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbell-40">[39]</a></sup> It is seen at change of command ceremonies and other public appearances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubbell_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbell-40">[39]</a></sup> A similar detachment is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_General%27s_Horse_Guards" title="Governor General's Horse Guards">Governor General's Horse Guards</a>, Canada's <a href="/wiki/Household_Cavalry" title="Household Cavalry">Household Cavalry</a> regiment, the last remaining mounted cavalry unit in the Canadian Forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>'s King's Household Cavalry is a ceremonial unit with over 100 horses and is the remainder of the Nepalese cavalry that existed since the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[219]</a></sup> An important ceremonial use is in military funerals, which often have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caparisoned_horse" title="Caparisoned horse">caparisoned horse</a> as part of the procession, "to symbolize that the warrior will never ride again".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>Horses are also used in many historical reenactments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[221]</a></sup> Reenactors try to recreate the conditions of the battle or tournament with equipment that is as authentic as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[222]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equestrian_sport">Equestrian sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Equestrian sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Equestrian at the Summer Olympics">Equestrian at the Summer Olympics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dressage" title="Dressage">Dressage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Show_jumping" title="Show jumping">Show jumping</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eventing" title="Eventing">Eventing</a></div>
<p>Modern-day Olympic equestrian events are rooted in cavalry skills and classical horsemanship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[223]</a></sup> The first equestrian events at the <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Equestrian at the Summer Olympics">Olympics</a> were introduced in 1912, and through 1948, competition was restricted to active-duty officers on military horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[224]</a></sup> Only after 1952, as mechanisation of warfare reduced the number of military riders, were civilian riders allowed to compete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[226]</a></sup> Dressage traces its origins to <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> and his works on cavalry training methods, developing further during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> in response to a need for different tactics in battles where firearms were used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[227]</a></sup> The three-phase competition known as <a href="/wiki/Eventing" title="Eventing">Eventing</a> developed out of cavalry officers' needs for versatile, well-schooled horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[228]</a></sup> Though <a href="/wiki/Show_jumping" title="Show jumping">show jumping</a> developed largely from <a href="/wiki/Fox_hunting" title="Fox hunting">fox hunting</a>, the cavalry considered jumping to be good training for their horses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[229]</a></sup> and leaders in the development of modern riding techniques over fences, such as <a href="/wiki/Federico_Caprilli" title="Federico Caprilli">Federico Caprilli</a>, came from military ranks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[230]</a></sup> Beyond the Olympic disciplines are other events with military roots. Competitions with weapons, such as <a href="/wiki/Cowboy_action_shooting" title="Cowboy action shooting">mounted shooting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tent_pegging" title="Tent pegging">tent pegging</a>, test the combat skills of mounted riders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[231]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Horses_in_warfare&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</ul>

<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Armouries" title="Royal Armouries">Royal Armouries</a> used a 15.2 hand <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Heavy_Draught" title="Lithuanian Heavy Draught">Lithuanian Heavy Draught</a> mare as a model for statues displaying various 15th and 16th century horse armour, as her body shape was an excellent fit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Possibly the <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kamboja</a> cavalry, from south of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> near medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohistan_District_(Pakistan)" title="Kohistan District (Pakistan)">Kohistan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmstead232_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olmstead232-112">[111]</a></sup></span></li>
